<div id="review_803736742" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/803736742" />
    <a title="Emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26734926-emma"><img alt="Emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565436013p2/26734926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/803736742?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma" href="/user/show/26734926-emma">Emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10128.The_Namesake">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26734926-emma?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26734926-emma?shelf=autumn-17">autumn-17</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer803736742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15413549442266861902">The Namesake follows a Bengali couple, who move to the USA in the 60s. Ashoke is a trained engineer, who quickly adapts to his new lifestyle. His wife Ashima deeply misses her family and struggles to adapt. Following the birth of her children, she pines for home even more.<br/><br/>Her two children grow up feeling more connected to America than India, and view their visits there as a chore. The elder child, Gogol is the main character. He struggles with his identity, and detests his unusual name. The book</span>
  <span id="freeText15413549442266861902" style="display:none">The Namesake follows a Bengali couple, who move to the USA in the 60s. Ashoke is a trained engineer, who quickly adapts to his new lifestyle. His wife Ashima deeply misses her family and struggles to adapt. Following the birth of her children, she pines for home even more.<br /><br />Her two children grow up feeling more connected to America than India, and view their visits there as a chore. The elder child, Gogol is the main character. He struggles with his identity, and detests his unusual name. The book follows this family over the period of about 30 years. We watch Gogol grow up, we see him fall in love, and we witness the family's shared tragedies. <br /><br />I very much enjoyed the subject matter. Ashima's culture shock and Gogol's identity crises both felt very authentic. I also liked seeing one family's experiences over such a large timescale. The one thing I didn't like was the narration style. It's written in the present tense, and the story somehow ended up feeling a little flat.<br /><br />It's probably an unpopular opinion, but I prefer Roopa Farooki's stories about second or third generation Asian families. That's probably an unfair comparison though, as they are generally more cheerful, lighter reads</span>
  <a data-text-id="15413549442266861902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1917655898?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phrynne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phrynne" href="/user/show/8655084-phrynne">Phrynne</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1917655898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9055204034797791846">This book tells a story which must be familiar to anyone who has migrated to another country - the fact that having made the transition to a new culture you are left missing the old and never quite achieving full admittance into the new. In fact a feeling of never quite belonging to either.<br/>This is the experience for Ashima and Ashoke Ganguli and it is probably made worse by the fact that India and America have such totally different cultures. The story follows their lives for 32 years from when </span>
  <span id="freeText9055204034797791846" style="display:none">This book tells a story which must be familiar to anyone who has migrated to another country - the fact that having made the transition to a new culture you are left missing the old and never quite achieving full admittance into the new. In fact a feeling of never quite belonging to either.<br />This is the experience for Ashima and Ashoke Ganguli and it is probably made worse by the fact that India and America have such totally different cultures. The story follows their lives for 32 years from when Ashima is pregnant and facing delivering her first child the American way without the comfort of her extended Indian family and all their social customs to help her.<br />Lahiri writes beautifully and the book is a pleasure to read. She also sees right to the heart of the issues of migrant families, from the mother who never adapts fully to the children who try to cast off their roots but find it very difficult to do.<br />My only issue was with the way the narrative rambles on, often about very insignificant issues yet passing too quickly over more important events. It was very well written rambling of course but my mind did occasionally wander away from the book. <br />Despite this, this is a beautiful book which tells a very important story and is well worth reading.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9055204034797791846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxwell" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell">Maxwell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=owned">owned</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1805755878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5538256180190612483"><b>3.5 stars</b> My favorite parts of any Jhumpa Lahiri story—whether it's a short story or novel—are her observations. She's so great creating realistic, emotionally-charged moments in her novels that feel so <i>true to life</i>. That being said, I think she excels at crafting narratives in the short story format. Both novels I've read from her have had wonderful and memorable moments but as a whole fall a little flat for me. The use of the third-person, present tense is also not my favorite because it convi</span>
  <span id="freeText5538256180190612483" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars</b> My favorite parts of any Jhumpa Lahiri story—whether it's a short story or novel—are her observations. She's so great creating realistic, emotionally-charged moments in her novels that feel so <i>true to life</i>. That being said, I think she excels at crafting narratives in the short story format. Both novels I've read from her have had wonderful and memorable moments but as a whole fall a little flat for me. The use of the third-person, present tense is also not my favorite because it convinces you that you are experiencing these things with the characters but you are held at a distance because you can't get inside their heads. I don't think it worked well here, and especially for a novel that deals a lot with nostalgia, traditions, and the past's effect on the present, I think the past tense would've worked better. That being said, I love Lahiri and will read anything she writes because scattered throughout her works are some incredible images, strong emotions, and lovely stories of families.</span>

<div id="review_1691607238" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1691607238" />
    <a title="Anna Luce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9429655-anna-luce"><img alt="Anna Luce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604227738p2/9429655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1691607238?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Luce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Luce" href="/user/show/9429655-anna-luce">Anna Luce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780358062684" href="/book/show/40796017-the-namesake">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of Elif Shafak &amp; Ann Patchett</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=favourite-relationships">favourite-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=physical-copies-i-own">physical-copies-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=titles-i-love">titles-i-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=4-favourites">4-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=poignant-reads">poignant-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=2020-reviews">2020-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9429655-anna-luce?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1691607238" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16940000306684406206">★★★★✰ 4.25 stars <br/><br/><blockquote>“In so many ways, his family's life feels like a string of accidents, unforeseen, unintended, one incident begetting another.”</blockquote><br/><br/>In the past few years I've read and fallen in love with Jhumpa Lahiri's collection of short stories as well as her book on her relationship with the Italian language <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25614298.In_Other_Words' title='In Other Words by Jhumpa Lahiri' rel='nofollow noopener'>In Other Words</a>. Although <i>The Namesake</i> has been sitting on my shelf for the last couple months, when it was chosen as one of the February reads for the 'Around the World in 80 Books' group, I </span>
  <span id="freeText16940000306684406206" style="display:none">★★★★✰ 4.25 stars <br /><br /><blockquote>“In so many ways, his family's life feels like a string of accidents, unforeseen, unintended, one incident begetting another.”</blockquote><br /><br />In the past few years I've read and fallen in love with Jhumpa Lahiri's collection of short stories as well as her book on her relationship with the Italian language <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25614298.In_Other_Words" title="In Other Words by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">In Other Words</a>. Although <i>The Namesake</i> has been sitting on my shelf for the last couple months, when it was chosen as one of the February reads for the 'Around the World in 80 Books' group, I was finally spurred into reading it, and I'm so glad I did. <i>The Namesake</i> did not disappoint.<br /><br />Written in an elegantly sparse prose <i>The Namesake</i> tells the story of the Ganguli family. After their arranged marriage Ashoke and Ashima Ganguili move from Calcutta to America. It is in this new, if not perpetually puzzling, country that their children Gogol and Sonia are born and raised.<br />As Lahiri recounts the story of this family, she also interrogates concepts of cultural identity, of dislocation and rootlessness, of cultural and generational divides, and of tradition and familial expectation. As the title of the novel suggests, <i>The Namesake </i>focuses on Gogol’s fraught relationship with his own name. As the American-born son of Bengali parents, Gogol struggles to reconcile himself with his Russian name. His uncommon name comes to symbolise his own self-divide and reticence to embrace his parents’ culture.<br /><br /><blockquote>“He wonders how his parents had done it, leaving their respective families behind, seeing them so seldom, dwelling unconnected, in a perpetual state of expectation, of longing.”</blockquote><br /><br />Names and trains are recurring motifs in this long spanning narrative. Time and again we read of the way in which names alter others’ and our perception of ourselves. Train journeys provide characters with life-changing experiences: from near misses with death to startling realisations.<br />Yet, in spite of these fated moments, Lahiri’s novel possesses an atmosphere that is at once graceful and ordinary. The language she chooses has this quiet quality that makes that which she writes all the more realistic. Her most insightful observations into her characters, or the dynamics between them, often occur when she is recounting seemingly mundane scenes: from food preparations and family meals to phone conversations.<br />In spite of the gentle rhythm of her narrative Lahiri also articulates the tension between past and present, India and America, parents and children, husband and wife. As Gogol grows we read of his love and sorrows, of his hopes and fears, and of his insecurities and his lifelong quest to belong. There are heartbreaking moments of affection and miscommunication, and Lahiri truly renders both the difficulties of acclimatising to another country and of embracing one's heritage in a world where to be different is to be other.<br /><br />By observing a characters’ clothes, appearance, or routine, Lahiri makes even those who are at the margin of the Ganguli’s family history come to life. The Ganguli's first neighbours in America, Gogol's teacher, who inadvertently cemented Gogol's hatred for his name, and even Moushumi's colleague are all vibrantly rendered. <br />While what Lahiri's characters' experience can be occasionally comic, she never makes them into a 'joke'. In fact, she reserves judgment, and each character, regardless of their actions, is portrayed with compassion. <br /><br /><blockquote>“True to the meaning of her name, she will be without borders, without a home of her own, a resident everywhere and nowhere.”</blockquote><br /><br />Another thing that makes this novel stand out is how much Lahiri leaves unspoken. There are no melodramatic scenes or confessions. At times it is only hindsight that allows a character to realise the importance of a certain moment. <br /><br /><blockquote>“Somehow, bad news, however ridden with static, however filled with echoes, always manages to be conveyed.”</blockquote><br /><br />There is a naturalness and openness to her characters' impressions. She writes with such clarity of such complex or ephemeral feelings or thoughts that I often had to stop to re-read a phrase in order to truly savour her words. <br /><br /><blockquote>“For being a foreigner, Ashima is beginning to realize, is a sort of lifelong pregnancy—a perpetual wait, a constant burden, a continuous feeling out of sorts. It is an ongoing responsibility, a parenthesis in what had once been ordinary life, only to discover that that previous life has vanished, replaced by something more complicated and demanding. Like pregnancy, being a foreigner, Ashima believes, is something that elicits the same curiosity from strangers, the same combination of pity and respect.”</blockquote><br /><br />Lahiri is a master of the trade and in <i>The Namesake</i> she depicts an exquisitely intricate family portrait.<br /><br /><a href="https://wishfullyreading.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Read more reviews on my blog</a> / / / <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/9429655-anna-luce" rel="nofollow noopener">View all my reviews on Goodreads</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16940000306684406206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_4720964" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4720964" />
    <a title="Sandhya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/215382-sandhya"><img alt="Sandhya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1244981990p2/215382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4720964?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandhya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandhya" href="/user/show/215382-sandhya">Sandhya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10128.The_Namesake">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4720964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10250965810844847761"><br/>It would only be fair to mention here that I saw Mira Nair's adaptation of the book before I actually got down to reading this novel recently. Having loved the film, I was keen to see how Lahiri had approached her characters and where its cinematic version stood in comparison.<br/><br/><br/>I'll say two things. First, I feel this is one of the few times when the film more than does justice to the book and second, that the book itself is a deeply involving and affecting experience. In fact, so compassionate and</span>
  <span id="freeText10250965810844847761" style="display:none"><br />It would only be fair to mention here that I saw Mira Nair's adaptation of the book before I actually got down to reading this novel recently. Having loved the film, I was keen to see how Lahiri had approached her characters and where its cinematic version stood in comparison.<br /><br /><br />I'll say two things. First, I feel this is one of the few times when the film more than does justice to the book and second, that the book itself is a deeply involving and affecting experience. In fact, so compassionate and compelling is the writer's understanding of her characters and their complexes, that the novel stays uniformly engaging till the very last page. Also, it helps that this is an extremely easy read and I for one, found myself going through it at a ravenous pace.<br /><br /><br />As a reader, one gets instantly drawn into the lives of young Ashima and Ashoke, who are a bundle of nerves in an alien country, far from adoring relatives and friends in Calcutta. The writer's description of how the couple grapples with the ways of a new world yet tightly holding on to their roots is deeply moving and rings true at every point.<br /><br /><br />When a letter from their grandmother in India, enclosing the name for their first born doesn't arrive in time, Ashoke instinctively and naively (as their son says later in life) names him Gogol- a name, derived from the Russian author, Nikolai Gogol, with whom the latter feels a deep connection. The name comes to embarrass their son as he grows older and is a reminder of his confused being -it's not even a proper Bengali name, he protests!<br /><br /><br />Gogol's agony is not so much about being born to Indian parents, as much as being saddled with a name that seems to convey nothing, in a way accentuating his feeling of "not really belonging to anything"<br />After much internal struggle, he changes his name to a more acceptable Indian name, Nikhil and feels it would enable him to face the world more confidently.<br /><br />But for me personally, the best part of the novel was Gogol's marriage to his childhood family friend Maushami Muzumdar. The latter is far from a conventional Bengali girl and Gogol is attracted to her individualistic streak and high living. In many ways, Maushami bridges a certain important gap in his mind and presents to him the best of both worlds --- she's Bengali like him, so in a strange way that's a comforting feeling. At the same time, she displays the same excessive, broadminded living of the Americans. <br /><br />However, the fact that this relationship collapses and leaves no mark in their individual lives whatsoever, is also a telling statement about how, ultimately, coming from a similar background provides no guarantee for marital success. On the other hand, his sister Sonia's marriage to an American proves to be quite blissful. <br /><br /><br />I've presented only an abridged version of my review but those with inclination to read further can see it my blog; <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.sandyi.blogspot.com">www.sandyi.blogspot.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10250965810844847761" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1954771007" />
    <a title="Usman Hickmath" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66366804-usman-hickmath"><img alt="Usman Hickmath" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532788781p2/66366804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1954771007?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Usman Hickmath" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Usman Hickmath" href="/user/show/66366804-usman-hickmath">Usman Hickmath</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1954771007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11088683510610465298">“Being a foreigner, is a sort of lifelong pregnancy—a perpetual wait, a constant burden, a continuous feeling out of sorts. It is an ongoing responsibility, a parenthesis in what had once been ordinary life, only to discover that that previous life has vanished, replaced by something more complicated and demanding. Like pregnancy, being a foreigner, is something that elicits the same curiosity from strangers, the same combination of pity and respect.” <br/><br/>Those lines vouch for how beautifully Jhumpa</span>
  <span id="freeText11088683510610465298" style="display:none">“Being a foreigner, is a sort of lifelong pregnancy—a perpetual wait, a constant burden, a continuous feeling out of sorts. It is an ongoing responsibility, a parenthesis in what had once been ordinary life, only to discover that that previous life has vanished, replaced by something more complicated and demanding. Like pregnancy, being a foreigner, is something that elicits the same curiosity from strangers, the same combination of pity and respect.” <br /><br />Those lines vouch for how beautifully Jhumpa Lahiri has portrayed the struggle of emigrants’ life in West. Her depiction of conflict of cultures faced by the second generation emigrants is interesting. <br /><br />But these MIT educated, middle class families’ struggles are completely different from what is being faced by the blue collar emigrant workers in Middle East and West. Would like to read a good work which represents them. Please recommend if you have read any on this area.</span>

You know, a commercial, populist work aimed to give you a flavor of India, shock you with arranged marriages, Indian family dynamics, struggles of Indian immigrants, etc., which at the same time gives you no real insight into the foreign mentality that isn't superficial or obvious. <br/><br/>Nothing new for me here.         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2642460736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10601049519378628435">The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri vividly describes the lives and the plight of the immigrant families, with a focus on Indians settled in America.<br/><br/>The book revolves around the common themes that this subject entails, mainly the immigrant experience as a whole, which includes the multi-cultured lives the families (especially the kids) lead, which then leads to being the basis of a queer relationship among the generations - the so called 'generation gap' which in this case is majorly affected by the c</span>
  <span id="freeText10601049519378628435" style="display:none">The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri vividly describes the lives and the plight of the immigrant families, with a focus on Indians settled in America.<br /><br />The book revolves around the common themes that this subject entails, mainly the immigrant experience as a whole, which includes the multi-cultured lives the families (especially the kids) lead, which then leads to being the basis of a queer relationship among the generations - the so called 'generation gap' which in this case is majorly affected by the culture clash.<br /><br />The story is emotional, and is sure to raise the hysteria in you. The bittersweet tale is sure to teach you a life lesson or two.<br /><br />This is my first read from Jhumpa, and I will be picking up more of her books in the future.<br /><br />Verdict: Recommended.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1039787706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11170374297608259025"><br/>So an Idaho School District is considering the possibility of banning <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33917.The_Namesake' title='The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Namesake</a> from their high schools reading list. <br/><br/>I don't know about other parents, but I trust that my kids are not going to read this beautiful novel and somehow plunge into a life of drug abuse...<br/><br/>Also, I might be mistaken since I read it a few years ago, but I don't recall that the use of recreational drugs is an essential part of the plot of this novel...<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.americanbazaaronline.com/2015/11/10/jhumpa-lahiris-the-namesake-recommended-by-idaho-school-district-to-be-removed-from-reading-list/'>http://www.americanbazaaronline.com/2...</a></span>
  <span id="freeText11170374297608259025" style="display:none"><br />So an Idaho School District is considering the possibility of banning <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33917.The_Namesake" title="The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">The Namesake</a> from their high schools reading list. <br /><br />I don't know about other parents, but I trust that my kids are not going to read this beautiful novel and somehow plunge into a life of drug abuse...<br /><br />Also, I might be mistaken since I read it a few years ago, but I don't recall that the use of recreational drugs is an essential part of the plot of this novel...<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.americanbazaaronline.com/2015/11/10/jhumpa-lahiris-the-namesake-recommended-by-idaho-school-district-to-be-removed-from-reading-list/">http://www.americanbazaaronline.com/2...</a></span>

<div id="review_3224220309" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3224220309" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3224220309?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=immigrants">immigrants</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=contemp-novel">contemp-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=paris">paris</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=life-stages">life-stages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=nyc">nyc</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3224220309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14352951757502245488"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33917.The_Namesake' title='The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Namesake</a> starts by introducing a Bengali couple, Ashoke and Ashima. Their marriage had been arranged by their parents, still customary in Bengali society in the 1960s. They are living in Cambridge, Massachusetts. Ashoke is pursuing a career in engineering. His wife, feeling isolated and alone, misses terribly friends and family in Calcutta, which she still considers “home”. In 1968, their first child is born. The story is primarily about him--we follow him through to his early thirties. <br/><br/>A ce</span>
  <span id="freeText14352951757502245488" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33917.The_Namesake" title="The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">The Namesake</a> starts by introducing a Bengali couple, Ashoke and Ashima. Their marriage had been arranged by their parents, still customary in Bengali society in the 1960s. They are living in Cambridge, Massachusetts. Ashoke is pursuing a career in engineering. His wife, feeling isolated and alone, misses terribly friends and family in Calcutta, which she still considers “home”. In 1968, their first child is born. The story is primarily about him--we follow him through to his early thirties. <br /><br />A central theme is “being an immigrant”. The importance of name is another theme. Ultimately, the book is about growing up, finding one’s own identity--a process that involves forging a life on one’s own terms. That life must also assimilate into it family ties and one’s heritage. <br /><br />Let’s consider the first theme--being an immigrant. In my youth I was an immigrant. Undeniably, this affects how I view the book. I see immigration as a challenge, a fantastic opportunity. Rather than bemoaning what you no longer have, see the opportunities that lie before you. There is so much new and exciting to see and to learn. Here is a chance to invent and create something new for yourself. Personally, I see the positive in the possibilities that lie before one.<br /><br />There are as many ways of seeing immigration as there are immigrants. It is interesting to note that Ashoke and Ashima view the immigrant experience differently. With time the new surrounding will inevitably alter one’s routines and perspectives. Ashima, for the sake of her two children, began to celebrate Christmas. I thought it wrong of her to seek out other Bengalis in America; one can choose to live in an area where one is forced to make new friends.<br /><br />Having immigrant parents leaves a stamp on the children’s lives too. They are squeezed between two cultures, the old and the new. Which will they choose? Do they fit comfortably in either? Does the experience make them stronger? <br /><br />The first portion of the novel, set in the 1960s and 1970s, felt for me like a trip down memory lane. My kids were raised in the 70s. This part I enjoyed; the author captured this time period well. The TV shows, the toys, the games kids played, the popular bands, the very way one viewed life then is drawn well. Lahiri has the details right. <br /><br />We watch as Ashoke’s and Ashima’s two children grow from kids, to teenagers to adults. The story follows more intensively the son and in particular his search to find a life partner. Will that woman be a true-blooded American type, or will he choose a woman of Bengali heritage? He tries both. We observe his relationships. Lining them up one against the other is amusing; comparing the different lifestyles makes one smile. You consider the good and the bad of both.<br /><br />In covering such a long time span and alternative lifestyles, the story becomes too long. My interest lagged. The tribulations typical of coming of age stories are drawn. Little is new. Furthermore, the telling focuses only on affluent and successful immigrants. The son goes to Yale and Colombia University and becomes a <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[successful architect <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />A theme covered that is not typical of coming of age tales is the importance of one’s name. How important is one’s name? Why is it so common to revolt against the name one is given, most often by our parents? What does this say about our relationship with our parents? What does it say about our desire to fit in? Or is the desire for a new name a way to stand out and be different? Don’t we all know kids who have insisted on changing their name? How long do the new names stick? How do we parents feel when our kids do this? What makes a person want to change their name? These questions intrigue me.<br /><br />I like the author’s prose style. The language is simple, but it captures well the atmosphere of a time and a place. There was one line which lyrically described a large leaf blown by the wind into the oncoming beam of a car. It spoke to me. Beautiful lines such as this make a difference to me; they enhance the reading experience. It is for this reason alone I will be reading more by the author. It is her prose style that I believe got her the 2000 Pulitzer Prize in fiction for her short story collection <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5439.Interpreter_of_Maladies" title="Interpreter of Maladies by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">Interpreter of Maladies</a>.<br /><br />I like and dislike different aspects of this novel. This is reflected in my rating.<br /><br />Sarita Choudbury narrates the audiobook. Her intonation of Indians speaking English is lovely to listen to. Off and on she reads too fast. Her narration I like and so have given it three stars.<br /><br />*****************<br /><br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33917.The_Namesake" title="The Namesake by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">The Namesake</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5439.Interpreter_of_Maladies" title="Interpreter of Maladies by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">Interpreter of Maladies</a> 2 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17262100.The_Lowland" title="The Lowland by Jhumpa Lahiri" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lowland</a> TBR</span>
  <a data-text-id="14352951757502245488" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_778867483" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/778867483" />
    <a title="Julie Ehlers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2120450-julie-ehlers"><img alt="Julie Ehlers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446217915p2/2120450.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/778867483?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Ehlers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Ehlers" href="/user/show/2120450-julie-ehlers">Julie Ehlers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2120450-julie-ehlers?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer778867483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18439685005336698934">Lahiri’s <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i> was a collection I admired more than I enjoyed, so I’m sorry to say I was apprehensive about reading her first full-length novel—but happy to report that it was an absolutely great experience. <i>The Namesake</i> is one of those books that works so well, so seamlessly, that it's hard to break it down into its various moving parts. I absolutely loved the characters (in fact, I flat-out longed for Gogol’s sister to have her own book, so intriguing did I find even the mino</span>
  <span id="freeText18439685005336698934" style="display:none">Lahiri’s <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i> was a collection I admired more than I enjoyed, so I’m sorry to say I was apprehensive about reading her first full-length novel—but happy to report that it was an absolutely great experience. <i>The Namesake</i> is one of those books that works so well, so seamlessly, that it's hard to break it down into its various moving parts. I absolutely loved the characters (in fact, I flat-out longed for Gogol’s sister to have her own book, so intriguing did I find even the minor players), and the emotional impact of the book, for me, was considerable. But what I may have loved the most was simply the use of good old-fashioned excellent storytelling in service of the theme of being caught between the country where your roots are and the country where you make your home. I don’t read enough novels like this, but that’s about to change.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18439685005336698934" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_7684530" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7684530" />
    <a title="Emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75857-emma"><img alt="Emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556514715p2/75857.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7684530?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma" href="/user/show/75857-emma">Emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7684530" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14272319899882594562">You've heard this story before. Junot Diaz, Julia Alvarez, Anzia Yezierska, and Edwidge Danticat are just a few of the authors who have told their own versions. The story they all have in common: The immigrant experience in the United States. Each of the above authors tackles this subject from a different enthnographic perspective, but the pull between the old (native) culture and the new (immigrant) one is always present.<br/><br/>Pulitzer prize winning author Jhumpa Lahiri adds to this conversation with</span>
  <span id="freeText14272319899882594562" style="display:none">You've heard this story before. Junot Diaz, Julia Alvarez, Anzia Yezierska, and Edwidge Danticat are just a few of the authors who have told their own versions. The story they all have in common: The immigrant experience in the United States. Each of the above authors tackles this subject from a different enthnographic perspective, but the pull between the old (native) culture and the new (immigrant) one is always present.<br /><br />Pulitzer prize winning author Jhumpa Lahiri adds to this conversation with "The Namesake" (her first novel which was a follow up to her short story collection "Interpreter of Maladies" which won the Pulitzer): the epic story of the Ganguli family's arrival and assimilation into the world of the United States.<br /><br />The story begins when Ashoke and his wife (of an arranged marriage), Ashima, come to Massachusetts where Ashoke is a graduate student at MIT. The year is 1968. At the beginning of the novel Ashima is pregnant with her first child, a son.<br /><br />In Bengali culture, it is common for people to have a formal name and a pet name (nickname). Ashoke has no problem coming up with a nickname for their son: Gogol. Unfortunately, due to a variety of mishaps and misunderstandings, the formal name proves harder to settle on and even harder to enforce. So Gogol Ganguli grows up with only a pet name--one that is not American, or Indian, or a first name.<br /><br />No one really cares that Gogol's name is so unique, except Gogol whose anxiety over his name is bothersome enough that no external taunts are necessary. Gogol eventually resolves to rename himself, but not after learning the life-changing story that inspired his father give Gogol his name in the first place.<br /><br />Despite the vast period Lahiri writes about, the novel's focus remains narrowly focused on the characters, especially Ashima and her son. Despite the authenticity that Lahiri brings to her main characters, certain scenes remain naggingly artificial--feeling simultaneously improbable and contrived.<br /><br />Lahiri's writing here (I've yet to read her short stories) is beautiful without being pretentious or overly self-aware. The story feels authentic and compelling despite the fact that so many of the cultural references remain worlds away.<br /><br />Even more interesting is the fact that I enjoyed almost the entire novel despite having a strong dislike of Gogol and several of the secondary characters. (I'd say more about what this means in terms of the writing style/skill but I still haven't figured out how that happened.) Despite my misgivings throughout the novel, Gogol does work toward redeeming himself by the end of the story.<br /><br />Regardless of my nitpicks, "The Namesake" remains a must for anyone interested in the immigrant experience in America. Lahiri's narrative hearkens back to Jeffrey Eugenides' "Middlesex" which has a similar scope, tracing three generation's relationship with Detroit.<br /><br />"The Namesake" deals with common themes but, as any good book should, Lahiri makes these subjects new and original with her unique characters and wonderful writing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14272319899882594562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_204153484" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/204153484" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/204153484?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007258918" href="/book/show/821872.The_Namesake">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2012)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-non-core">1001-non-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=india">india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer204153484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8030846157661248344">No second thought at all: this book is well-written.<br/><br/>It has all the elements of a good novel: tight intriguing plot, show don't tell, memorable characters that you can't help but empathize with and it teaches us a thing or two about being marginalized if not discriminated or alienated because we are different from most of the people we find ourselves with. I am living in the country where I was born but I have two siblings who are now living in the West (older brother in California and older sist</span>
  <span id="freeText8030846157661248344" style="display:none">No second thought at all: this book is well-written.<br /><br />It has all the elements of a good novel: tight intriguing plot, show don't tell, memorable characters that you can't help but empathize with and it teaches us a thing or two about being marginalized if not discriminated or alienated because we are different from most of the people we find ourselves with. I am living in the country where I was born but I have two siblings who are now living in the West (older brother in California and older sister in Winnipeg) and I have heard sorry stories about their struggles to fit in when they were starting to live in those far foreign countries. My 78-y/o mother is also living alone in a senior's apartment in National City, CA and she's been there for almost twenty years now and there are more Filipinos in that senior's apartment so she says that sometimes she feels that she is still in the Philippines with the TFG (The Filipino Channel) on whenever she is awake and inside her room.<br /><br />I am sure my brother, sister and my mom felt the same way as the couple <b>Ashoke</b> and <b>Ashimi</b> in this first novel by Jhumpa Lahiri (born 1967). Especially since my brother and my sister-in-law have two kids although their first born is called Jonathan (taken from the OT because my brother is deeply religious but not the religious type who quotes biblical verses or maintains a religious blog as a facade to hide an evil lurking inside his sheep's clothing) and not after an Asian writer like Jose Rizal, Haruki Murakami or Mo Yan. In the case of the couple in this book, they named their firstborn <b>Gogol</b> from the Russian writer Nicholai Gogol (1809-1852) because Ashoke was saved from the train accident while reading a book by that author. Lahiri drew inspiration from her own experience as her real name is Nilanjana Sudeshna and when she was in grade school, her teacher just called her "Jhumpa" as it was easier to pronounce. Lahiri recalled, <i>"I always felt so embarrassed by my name.... You feel like you're causing someone pain just by being who you are."</i> So, Gogol, the main protagonist in the book refuses to understand the simple reason for his name and denies his heritage as an Bengali and when he grows up he constantly struggles to shed anything Indian in himself to the point of ignoring his family. Contrast is provided by Lahiri in the person of Gogol's younger sister named <b>Sonia</b> as she has an English name thereby breezing through in trying to assimilate with white people. What is in the name? Everything. We use it everyday - in the office we wear our IDs hanging by our necks, our cubicles have our names, our emails have our signatures by default. That's why when my daughter was born, I and my wife really made sure that we chose the best name for her - not too traditional, not old-sounding, not to western but also not after some writers or movie actress.<br /><br />There is almost nothing to dislike about this book. It's just that I did not have any first-hand experience of rearing a child in another country. I had some experiences when I felt discriminated during my trips abroad particularly in the Western countries but they were just some events and did not really humiliated me bigtime. Yet, we don't really need to have similar experiences of our own in order to appreciate good stories, right? <br /><br />Good writing is good writing regardless of whether we could relate to the story or not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8030846157661248344" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_9777'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things"><img alt="The God of Small Things" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1590282886l/9777._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8W0QsLjn4+/7pGa7sGPNVniXvVrZI0ly4CAHkDkqSPkAs/22oEj3Vym8jH+IhldVm1wEcbGUgQeIzZBdkzSFtA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('T8VPgeRK8xdUk0fQIwk52GegbbzSQboxPxFeG3JuHiK+G6KH/OXnr4aLrRQb7KPbhGvUl7r2ckRX/MnW2HDTbw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Balram learns how to siphon gas, deal with corrupt mechanics, and refill and resell Johnnie Walker Black Label bottles (all but one). He also finds a way out of the Coop that no one else inside it can perceive. \n\nBalram\'s eyes penetrate India as few outsiders can: the cockroaches and the call centers; the prostitutes and the worshippers; the ancient and Internet cultures; the water buffalo and, trapped in so many kinds of cages that escape is (almost) impossible, the white tiger.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3670.Jhumpa_Lahiri">About Jhumpa Lahiri</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5739140474720835450">Nilanjana Sudeshna "Jhumpa" Lahiri was born in London and brought up in South Kingstown, Rhode Island. Brought up in America by a mother who wanted to raise her children to be Indian, she learned about her Bengali heritage from an early age. <br/><br/>Lahiri graduated from South Kingstown High School and later received her B.A. in English literature from Barnard College in 1989. She then received multiple d</span>
  <span id="freeText5739140474720835450" style="display:none">Nilanjana Sudeshna "Jhumpa" Lahiri was born in London and brought up in South Kingstown, Rhode Island. Brought up in America by a mother who wanted to raise her children to be Indian, she learned about her Bengali heritage from an early age. <br /><br />Lahiri graduated from South Kingstown High School and later received her B.A. in English literature from Barnard College in 1989. She then received multiple degrees from Boston University: an M.A. in English, an M.A. in Creative Writing, an M.A. in Comparative Literature and a Ph.D. in Renaissance Studies. She took up a fellowship at Provincetown's Fine Arts Work Center, which lasted for the next two years (1997-1998).<br /><br />In 2001, she married Alberto Vourvoulias-Bush, a journalist who was then Deputy Editor of <i>TIME Latin America</i> Lahiri currently lives in Brooklyn with her husband and two children. She has been a Vice President of the PEN American Center since 2005.<br /><br />Lahiri taught creative writing at Boston University and the Rhode Island School of Design. Much of her short fiction concerns the lives of Indian-Americans, particularly Bengalis.<br /><br />She received the following awards, among others:<br />1999 - PEN/Hemingway Award (Best Fiction Debut of the Year) for <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i>;<br />2000 - The New Yorker's Best Debut of the Year for <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i>;<br />2000 - Pulitzer Prize for Fiction for her debut <i>Interpreter of Maladies</i> </span>
  <a data-text-id="5739140474720835450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
